14:I[8669,["/paper-daily-site/_next/static/chunks/0u8r51s9xyykl.js","/paper-daily-site/_next/static/chunks/0d3shmwh5_nmn.js","/paper-daily-site/_next/static/chunks/0eajwm.uf2h.~.js","/paper-daily-site/_next/static/chunks/0qg9yqbp9fkn8.js","/paper-daily-site/_next/static/chunks/06c8.1p7kdsm~.js"],"DateSidebar"]
15:I[44692,["/paper-daily-site/_next/static/chunks/0u8r51s9xyykl.js","/paper-daily-site/_next/static/chunks/0d3shmwh5_nmn.js","/paper-daily-site/_next/static/chunks/0eajwm.uf2h.~.js","/paper-daily-site/_next/static/chunks/0qg9yqbp9fkn8.js","/paper-daily-site/_next/static/chunks/06c8.1p7kdsm~.js"],"DailyView"]
16:T4b7,They propose CSAttention (Centroid-Scoring Attention), a training-free sparse attention method built around a storage-for-computation tradeoff.
- Offline prefill (one-time, amortized): split the feature space into m subspaces; cluster prefill queries in each subspace into C centroids. For each centroid, precompute partial dot-products with all keys in that subspace (centroid-scores) and store a compressed, bounded Top-L list (key indices + scores).
- Online decode (per step): for a new query, pick the nearest centroid in each subspace (1-of-C), fetch the m short lists, and accumulate partial scores by key index on GPU. Keys that align with the query tend to score well across multiple subspaces and rise to the top after accumulation; then select Top-k keys for sparse attention.
- Incremental maintenance: as new keys arrive during decoding, attempt to insert them into the relevant centroids’ Top-L lists only if their partial score exceeds the current minimum, keeping capacity bounded and update overhead small.
The key idea is query-centric indexing to mitigate Q/K distribution shift, plus regular, GPU-friendly data structures/kernels to avoid full-context scans and irregular control flow.17:T63a,must_read (deep technical analysis + what to focus on)
1) Introduction: lock in the serving model (offline prefill vs online decode) and the argument that KV/bandwidth dominates; note Q/K distribution shift as the core failure mode at high sparsity.
2) Method: study (i) subspace partitioning (m) and centroid clustering (C), (ii) centroid-score precomputation and the compressed Top-L table layout, and (iii) the online accumulation + Top-k selection pipeline and why it maps to regular GPU kernels.
3) Incremental updates: understand how new keys update tables, the complexity dependence on m/C, and why overhead stays negligible.
4) Key figures: prioritize quality–sparsity–throughput curves and offline build cost vs amortization (break-even reuse).
5) Ablations: query-centric vs key-centric, necessity of subspace aggregation, Top-L capacity effects, and robustness under long-generation query drift.
Main contributions: (A) query-centric offline indexing for reusable prefill, (B) centroid-score + subspace accumulation for high-sparsity retrieval, (C) bounded Top-L tables with lightweight incremental maintenance, (D) GPU-friendly online execution without full-context scans.

skim (what to read)
- Read the Introduction for the scenario and why prior sparse/index methods degrade at high sparsity.
- Look at the method overview figure/pseudocode (two-phase: prefill build vs decode retrieval).
- Skim the main results at 90–95% sparsity (quality retention and throughput gains) and baseline comparisons.
- Check recommended m/C/L settings and sensitivity for practical adoption.18:T73e,must_read (deep technical reading)
- Focus sections:
  1) Introduction: motivation, community statistics (19.2%), and the real impact case (OLMo non-convergence; Figure 1).
  2) Empirical study section: how the 116 bugs are collected/validated; the 3-type/6-subtype taxonomy; root causes and triggering patterns—this is the paper’s core knowledge contribution.
  3) SOTA testing evaluation: benchmark setup, correctness oracle (compiled vs eager), and the analysis explaining the 33.8% ceiling (especially why graph-related bugs are entirely missed).
  4) AlignGuard: the end-to-end loop from triggering patterns to mutation operators/prompts/constraints, test filtering, and differential validation.
- Key figures/tables to inspect:
  - Figure 1 (motivation/impact): demonstrates real training degradation due to silent correctness bugs.
  - Distribution of bug types/subtypes (notably in-place at 21.6%).
  - Detection-rate comparison across five techniques (overall 33.8%) and per-category miss analysis.
- Innovation checkpoints:
  1) First systematic empirical study dedicated to torch.compile silent correctness bugs under dynamic/JIT graph capture.
  2) First targeted evaluation of SOTA DL compiler testing methods on torch.compile, with a trigger-pattern-based explanation of systematic misses.
  3) AlignGuard: LLM-driven, trigger-pattern-guided test mutation validated by 23 new real bugs.

skim (high-level reading)
- Recommended order: Introduction (problem + stats) → contributions → taxonomy results (3 types/6 subtypes + top root causes) → headline evaluation result (33.8% and graph-related = 0%) → AlignGuard idea + outcomes (23 new bugs; 14 high-priority).
- Can skim: detailed torch.compile pipeline background unless you implement compilers; long tail of individual bug case narratives unless building a regression suite.19:T4d6,The Fast Feedforward (FFF) layer replaces the dense MLP with P parallel perfect binary trees of depth D. For each input token x, each tree performs D hard binary routing decisions from root to leaf, evaluating exactly one node per level; thus each tree activates a single path and the layer activates exactly P×D nodes. Each node has (1) routing parameters (w_in, b_in) producing a scalar logit used for a hard left/right decision, with stop-gradient used around the discrete decision; and (2) output parameters (w_out) producing a contribution vector. The layer output is the sum of the output vectors from all visited nodes along the path (internal nodes both route and contribute). Compared to MoE, there is no separate router network, no top-k expert aggregation, and sparsity is hierarchical/structured.
A key finding is an emergent auto-pruning effect: the interaction of hard routing and asymmetric nonlinearities progressively deactivates entire branches during training, partially converting input-dependent dynamic sparsity into stable structural sparsity. The paper argues this behavior is controllable via simple architectural choices, helping manage routing imbalance and tree utilization without auxiliary balancing losses.1a:T4b0,must_read (deep read)
1) Introduction: focus on why FFN is the compute bottleneck and why MoE is operationally complex; understand the motivation for structured tree sparsity.
2) Fast Feedforward Layer section: carefully parse the node parameterization, the hard routing update with stop-gradient, and the “sum of visited nodes” output rule—this defines how FFF differs from MoE/gated MLPs.
3) Autoregressive LM results: prioritize Fig.2 (loss curves) and check how sparsity/compute/parameter matching to MoE is defined.
4) Auto-pruning analysis: read the figures showing utilization collapse over training and the ablations tying it to architectural choices; this is contribution (ii).
5) Runtime benchmarks: examine measurement methodology and where overheads come from (branching, memory access, kernel fusion), since theoretical sparsity may not translate to wall-clock speed.

skim (quick read)
- Read Introduction + Fig.1 to build the mental model of FFF.
- Jump to the main LM result figure (Fig.2) to validate “high sparsity without loss.”
- Skim the auto-pruning key plots and conclusion to understand the extra structural sparsity story and its risks (routing collapse/imbalance).1b:T416,must_read (deep read)
1) Introduction: internalize the core FP4 failure mode (outliers → block scaling → underflow/bias) and why SR/RHT help.
2) HiF4 format section: understand the three-level scaling, bit allocations, and 64-element block choice; compare representable range/scaling granularity to MXFP4.
3) FP4 training pipeline: track which ops run in FP4 vs FP16/FP32, and where RHT/SR/(MXFP4) truncation-free scaling are inserted and what they cost.
4) Main results tables/figures: focus on loss-gap comparisons across 1B/8B/30B-MoE and how “~90% FP4” is measured (FLOPs, kernels, or time).
5) Hardware/system discussion: Ascend NPU execution details that enable FP4 GEMM and constraints that force higher-precision steps—this determines portability.

skim (quick read)
- Read Abstract + early Introduction for the motivation and the outlier/gradient-bias issue.
- Jump to the HiF4 vs MXFP4 headline results (loss gaps and required stabilization techniques).
- Skim the pipeline diagram/description to see where RHT/SR are applied.1c:T726,(must_read: detailed technical analysis)
1) Geometric framing: Pretraining minimizes a weighted average of K source losses. The paper distinguishes two solution types that achieve the same L_train: Sum-type (only optimal for the aggregate) vs Intersection-type (geometrically close to each source optimum). The key quantity is “closeness,” treated as a second-order property tied to curvature/Hessian structure.
2) Theoretical motivation (quadratic/i.i.d.): Under simplified quadratic losses with i.i.d. task distribution, the paper states a result (Theorem 2.2) that, at the same pretraining loss, Intersection-type minimizers strictly outperform Sum-type minimizers on a downstream task drawn from the same distribution.
3) Tractable proxy: Directly optimizing closeness is intractable because it would require knowing each source’s exact minimizer during training. The authors argue/prove that gradient cosine similarity CosSim(∇L_i, ∇L_j) upper-bounds closeness: if gradient directions across sources are always aligned, their minimizers must coincide (or be very close).
4) Nexus algorithm: Without changing the pretraining objective, Nexus introduces an optimizer-level mechanism to maximize cross-source gradient cosine similarity. It approximates and uses ∇CosSim(∇L_i, ∇L_j) (involving second-order information / gradients of gradients) and combines it with standard pretraining optimizers (e.g., Adam/Muon) to bias updates toward more aligned gradients, thereby steering convergence toward Intersection-type common minima.
5) Mechanistic link to downstream: In Sec. 5.1, the authors claim that increased gradient similarity and geometric closeness not only hold among training sources but also transfer to downstream tasks, yielding lower downstream loss and better metrics at identical pretraining loss.1d:T559,(must_read: deep reading)
1) Introduction + Fig. 2: essential. Fig. 2 defines the Sum vs Intersection geometry that anchors the entire paper.
2) “Closeness: A Second-Order Property Related to Generalization”: focus on the formal definition of closeness, why it is second-order, and how it is connected to downstream generalization.
3) Theory section (incl. Theorem 2.2): verify assumptions (quadratic, i.i.d., downstream drawn from same distribution) and interpret the guarantee as motivation vs transferable theorem.
4) Nexus method details: how ∇CosSim is approximated/computed (HVP? extra forward/backward? how many source pairs), and how it is integrated with the base optimizer.
5) Sec. 5.1 (mechanistic evidence): scrutinize how they measure gradient similarity/closeness, how they align “same pretraining loss” comparisons, and whether alternative explanations (regularization/tuning) are ruled out.
6) Results tables: prioritize comparisons matched by pretraining loss (not steps/tokens), since the thesis is loss-equal but generalization-different.
(skim: quick pass)
- Read Introduction + Fig. 2, the high-level Nexus description (maximize cross-source gradient cosine similarity), and the main downstream results (especially 3B on GSM8K/MATH/HumanEval).
- If you care about practicality, skim the compute/memory overhead and implementation details.1e:T68e,(must_read: detailed technical analysis)
1) Key observation (linear-layer gradients lie in activation span): for a linear layer, the weight gradient is G_W = X^T G_out, hence it lies in the span/column space of input activations X. Therefore, if activations are approximated in a rank-r subspace, gradients admit an optimal rank-r representation in the same subspace.
2) Compress activations while keeping forward exact: OASIS does not modify forward-pass computation. It only projects and stores intermediate activations for backward:
- Store \tilde X_t = X_t U_t (N×r) instead of X_t (N×d).
- Perform gradient/optimizer computations in the low-rank space and project updates back via U_t.
3) Joint low-rank gradients and optimizer states: since gradients are kept as (r×m), Adam’s first/second moments can be stored and updated in the same low-rank space, reducing memory from O(dm) to O(rm) per layer.
4) Online subspace learning (core novelty): U_t cannot be fixed because activation distributions drift as parameters change. The paper uses Oja’s rule (streaming PCA) to update U_t incrementally without repeated exact eigendecomposition.
5) Projection-aware state updates: when U_t changes, low-rank momentum/variance states must be transformed to remain consistent with the new basis; otherwise optimization becomes unstable. Following LDAdam, they apply projection-aware alignment of optimizer states after subspace updates.
6) Cost/benefit: per layer, activation storage drops Nd→Nr; gradients and optimizer states drop dm→rm. Extra overhead comes from maintaining U_t and projections, aiming for large peak-memory savings with acceptable throughput/accuracy.1f:T4e9,(must_read: deep reading)
1) Fig. 1(a)(b): understand memory breakdown and the empirical subspace drift argument; Fig. 1(b) is central to why online updates matter.
2) Methodology derivations: verify why G_W = X^T G_out implies gradients live in the activation span and why the projected form yields the optimal rank-r gradient approximation.
3) Algorithm 1 (if present): read the end-to-end loop—when U_t is updated, what is stored, and how backward uses \tilde X_t and U_t.
4) Oja’s rule details: step size, orthonormalization frequency, and stability tricks; these govern drift tracking and training stability.
5) Projection-aware optimizer states: a key engineering point—how moments are transformed when U_t changes and what happens without it; compare to LDAdam.
6) Ablations: prioritize fixed vs online subspace, varying rank r, per-layer vs shared ranks, and peak-memory curves under long context/large batch.
(skim: quick pass)
- Read Introduction (motivation/contributions), the first half of Methodology (activation projection → low-rank gradients/states), and the main result tables (memory vs accuracy).
- For deployability, skim implementation notes (custom kernels, throughput impact, compatibility with FlashAttention/checkpointing).20:T7a5,(must_read technical analysis)
1) Likelihood-based formulation: the paper casts order selection as minimizing the gap between (i) a practical permutation-based factorization likelihood (tokens predicted following a chosen order) and (ii) a permutation-independent likelihood where each token is predicted conditioned on all other target tokens. The objective is to minimize (an upper bound of) the log-likelihood gap, directly tying order choice to likelihood maximization.

2) Main theoretical result: under a one-layer Transformer with softmax self-attention, the paper shows the gap upper bound is governed by attention structure—specifically attention column sums. Intuitively, a token with a large attention column sum is broadly attended to and structurally central.
- Result: decoding tokens in descending order of attention column sums approximately minimizes the gap upper bound, hence approximately maximizes target log-likelihood.
This provides a formal bridge between self-attention structure and optimal decoding order.

3) Attn-Sampler (training-free): the algorithm operationalizes the theory by using the model’s attention matrix (rather than output probabilities) to decide which tokens are most informative to decode at each step.
- Scalability: a block attention approximation avoids computing the full attention matrix and stays compatible with efficient attention kernels.
- Parallelism control: dynamic attention thresholding adaptively adjusts how many tokens to decode per step based on sequence-wide dependency strength, improving parallel decoding without sacrificing quality.

4) Theoretical comparison to token-level samplers: the framework explains why confidence/entropy/margin heuristics can deviate from likelihood maximization—they capture local uncertainty but not global structural importance, potentially decoding key “hub” tokens too late or spending steps on locally easy but globally less critical positions.21:T4f3,must_read
- Key sections:
  1) Introduction: problem statement (order selection) and limitations of token-level greedy decoding.
  2) Theory section: definitions of the two likelihoods, the log-likelihood gap, and the derivation linking the gap bound to attention column sums (core contribution).
  3) Attn-Sampler method: how attention induces ordering/parallel token sets, the dynamic thresholding rule, and the block attention approximation for efficiency.
  4) Experiments: focus on head-to-head comparisons vs confidence/entropy/margin samplers and speed–quality trade-off plots.
- Key figures/tables:
  - The main theorem/inequality showing why column-sum ordering is near-optimal.
  - Plots showing how dynamic thresholding changes decoded-token count per step and impacts quality.
- Innovation checklist:
  1) Likelihood-gap minimization formulation for order selection;
  2) Near-optimality of attention column-sum ordering;
  3) Training-free, scalable Attn-Sampler (block approximation + adaptive thresholding).

skim
- Read Introduction + method overview (algorithm/pseudocode) + main comparison tables.
- In theory, keep only the takeaway: “descending attention column sums ≈ closer to maximum likelihood than token-level uncertainty heuristics.”22:T601,(must_read technical analysis)
1) Empirical diagnosis and three insights:
(I) Naive lookahead is unreliable: even stable tokens can exhibit temporary fluctuations, so local one-step (or short-horizon) confidence checks can misjudge stability.
(II) Stability correlates with a convergence trend: after the first stable step, stable tokens show a sharp, fast-growing confidence trajectory termed the absolute stability trend. Thus stability should be defined via trajectory trends rather than single-point thresholds.
(III) History is isolated in standard decoding: each step depends mainly on the previous step and the final output uses only the last step, leaving earlier trajectory information unused for stability decisions.

2) AHD (Anchor-based History-stable Decoding), training-free and plug-and-play:
- At each step, treat the current step as a dynamic anchor and retrospectively examine a historical buffer of prior steps.
- Use a history-consistency / trend-consistency criterion to detect the emergence of the absolute stability trend early, while being robust to transient fluctuations.
- Once identified, cross-block stable tokens are unlocked early, effectively breaking block boundaries.

3) Theoretical support: the paper claims a proof that the history-based criterion enables earlier and more reliable stability detection under certain conditions, reducing erroneous early unlocking. When reading, align the proof assumptions with the empirical patterns shown in Figure 2 (trend vs peak; windowing filters fluctuations).23:T410,must_read
- Key sections:
  1) Introduction with Fig. 1: understand Block-Boundary Delay and radiative effects.
  2) Stability analysis with Fig. 2: why naive lookahead fails and what “absolute stability trend” means.
  3) AHD details: dynamic anchor, historical buffer/window, the exact stability criterion, and integration into Semi-AR (when/how many tokens to unlock).
  4) Theory: map the proof to observed trajectory behavior.
  5) Experiments: cross-domain consistency and ablations (no history / no anchor / window size).
- Innovation checklist:
  1) Systematic characterization of Semi-AR block-boundary delay and cross-block stable tokens;
  2) Trend-based stability definition and the “history isolation” diagnosis;
  3) AHD: dynamic anchor + history-based stability criterion, training-free and plug-and-play.

skim
- Read Fig. 1–2 + method pseudocode/overview + main result tables.
- Skim the theory; keep the intuition: use historical trajectories to detect stable tokens robustly and unlock them early across blocks.24:Tc3a,（must_read 级别技术分析）
1) 问题形式化与关键指标：
- AQLM 将每个 g 维权重组 w∈R^g 表示为 M 个码本向量之和：ŵ=∑_{m=1..M} c_{m,b_m}，每个码本 C_m∈R^{K×g}，K=256。2 bpp（g=8）常用 M=2（两个 8-bit 索引）。
- 层级目标不是最小化权重误差，而是最小化校准激活 X 上的输出重建误差（作者引用其与困惑度增量线性相关的结论）：本质是让量化后的线性层输出尽量匹配 FP16。
- 提出“表征比（representational ratio）”ρ=N/K^M，其中 N 为该层 weight group 数量，K^M 为组合码字容量。ρ<1 为 overcomplete（容量富余），ρ>1 为 undercomplete（容量稀缺、组间竞争）。作者强调从 3 bpp 到 2 bpp 会使 K^M 发生数量级变化，从而触发行为的“相变”：初始化从次要因素变为决定性因素。

2) 失败机理：贪心顺序初始化的“过早承诺（premature commitment）”
- 标准残差/顺序策略：先拟合第一个码本（或先选第一个码字），再在残差上拟合第二个码本；或在分配时逐码本贪心选择最优条目且不回溯。
- 在 ρ>1 的 undercomplete 区间，码字组合容量不足以覆盖所有 group 的多样性，早期错误选择会占用稀缺码字资源并造成后续码本无法补偿；即使后续用 beam search 扩大搜索，也往往是在“坏 basin”附近做局部改进。
- 论文给出强烈的经验现象：在 Llama 3.2 3B 上，2 bpp 时贪心初始化导致极高困惑度（例如 beam=4 时 WT2 PPL 352.39；beam=8 仍 60.61；beam=16 也仅降到 46.01），表现为典型的“初始化灾难”。

3) 核心方法：OA-EM（Output-Aware EM）初始化
- 目标：把初始化从“权重空间距离/残差 k-means”转为“输出误差最小化”，直接对齐层级目标。
- 方法：对每个码本做 EM 式迭代初始化/细化，E 步更新分配（或软/硬责任），M 步更新码字；关键在于距离度量采用由校准数据导出的 Hessian 加权 Mahalanobis 距离（可理解为二阶敏感度加权的输出空间度量），让对输出更敏感的方向被更强约束。
- 直觉：在输出误差目标下，最该优先拟合的是“对输出影响大”的权重子空间；Hessian 加权相当于把误差从参数空间拉回到函数空间（层输出）并做二阶近似，从而得到更“任务对齐”的初始化 basin。

4) 与后续优化的关系：初始化决定 basin，而非仅决定起点
- 作者强调 OA-EM 是可替换 greedy 初始化的 drop-in 模块，后续仍可接 beam search 与 PV-tuning。
- 关键结论不是 OA-EM 单次更好，而是其优势在后续搜索/微调后仍“盆地持久（basin persistence）”：说明初始化改变了可达解集合与优化轨迹，而不是被后续训练完全抹平。

（skim 级别概述）
- 提出 ρ=N/K^M 解释 2-bit 下为何对初始化极敏感。
- 用 OA-EM（输出感知 + Hessian 加权 Mahalanobis）替代贪心/残差 k-means 初始化，显著提升 2 bpp 可用性，并在 beam search 与 PV-tuning 后仍保持优势。25:Ta4f,(must_read technical analysis)
1) Formulation and the key regime indicator:
- Additive quantization represents each g-dimensional weight group w as a sum of M codewords: ŵ=∑_{m=1..M} c_{m,b_m}, with codebooks C_m∈R^{K×g}, K=256. At 2 bpp with g=8, AQLM typically uses M=2 (two 8-bit indices per group).
- The layer-wise objective minimizes output reconstruction error on calibration activations X (rather than weight-space error), which prior work shows correlates linearly with perplexity increase.
- The paper introduces the representational ratio ρ=N/K^M, where N is the number of weight groups in a layer and K^M is the combinatorial capacity of codeword sums. ρ<1 is overcomplete (capacity surplus), ρ>1 is undercomplete (capacity scarce, groups compete), and the transition to 2 bpp can trigger a qualitative regime change.

2) Why greedy sequential initialization breaks at ρ>1:
- Residual / sequential fitting (or greedy per-codebook assignment without revisiting earlier choices) suffers from premature commitment: the locally best choice for the first codebook may pair poorly with the remaining codebooks.
- In the undercomplete regime, early mistakes consume scarce representational capacity and become effectively irrecoverable; wider beam search often only explores locally around a bad basin.
- Empirically, on Llama 3.2 3B at 2 bpp, greedy initialization yields extreme perplexity (e.g., WT2 PPL 352.39 at beam 4; 60.61 at beam 8; still 46.01 at beam 16), illustrating the “initialization catastrophe.”

3) OA-EM initialization:
- OA-EM (Output-Aware EM) replaces greedy initialization with an EM-style refinement that directly targets output reconstruction error.
- The key ingredient is a Hessian-weighted Mahalanobis distance derived from calibration activations, effectively emphasizing directions that matter most for the layer’s output (a second-order, function-space-aligned metric).
- OA-EM is designed as a drop-in replacement for the initialization stage; beam search and PV-tuning remain unchanged downstream.

4) Initialization determines the basin, not just the starting point:
- The paper’s central claim is basin persistence: OA-EM’s advantage survives extensive downstream search and end-to-end PV-tuning, indicating initialization changes the reachable solution region/trajectory.

(skim-level overview)
- Use ρ=N/K^M to predict when quantization becomes initialization-sensitive.
- Replace greedy/residual k-means initialization with OA-EM (output-aware, Hessian-weighted EM) to stabilize and improve 2 bpp additive quantization, with gains persisting after beam search and PV-tuning.26:T585,must_read (deep dive + what to focus on)
- §3.3: The representational ratio ρ=N/K^M and the overcomplete/undercomplete regime transition; this is the paper’s main explanatory lens.
- §3.4: OA-EM details—EM procedure, how the Hessian-weighted Mahalanobis metric is constructed from calibration activations, and how it aligns initialization with the output-error objective.
- §5.2 (and related ablations/tables): Post-PV comparisons demonstrating basin persistence, plus the beam-width scaling results showing asymmetric effects.
- Key figures/tables to prioritize:
  1) The 2 bpp “catastrophe” results for greedy initialization across beam widths.
  2) Cross-model/setting post-PV summaries showing OA-EM remains better.
  3) Any compute–quality Pareto plots/tables supporting the dominance claim.
- Innovations to extract:
  1) ρ as a predictor of initialization sensitivity;
  2) OA-EM as an output-aware, Hessian-weighted EM initializer;
  3) Systematic evidence that initialization determines the optimization basin.

1) Controlled architecture: attention-only + frozen representation layers
- Built on the GPT-2 117M design/tokenizer, but only ~2.4M parameters are trainable: token embeddings and the output projection are frozen, and MLP sublayers are removed so learning is concentrated in self-attention.
- Motivation for freezing: GPT-2 ties input embeddings and output projection weights. In tiny/data-limited regimes, training embeddings can dominate learning, cause rapid overfitting, and confound dataset-size effects. Freezing stabilizes optimization and keeps the representation space fixed.

2) Dataset scaling protocol: power-of-two subsets + robustness checks
- Train on progressively larger power-of-two token subsets spanning ~0.1M to 134M tokens, covering strongly data-limited regimes up to token/parameter ratios near common heuristics.
- Analyze subset representativeness via token-distribution checks and run multi-seed experiments to assess robustness.

3) Metrics and scaling-law alignment
- Track validation token-level accuracy (and training dynamics) as data increases, looking for smooth monotonic gains and diminishing returns consistent with scaling-law intuition.

(skim-level overview)
- Train a tiny decoder where only attention is learned, vary only dataset size.
- Observe smooth improvements with diminishing returns; ~30% of the data reaches ~90% of full-data validation accuracy.28:T4b5,must_read (deep dive)

skim (fast path)

- Jump to the main scaling curves and the conclusion highlighting the ~30%→~90% result and diminishing returns trend.29:T813,(must_read: detailed technical analysis)
1) Model class and restrictions: The authors analyze a restricted yet practical MoE Transformer family: fixed depth/width submodules, deterministic top-k routing, uniformly bounded weights, and a routing family expressive enough to implement a k-sparse partition of unity. Each block has non-expert (attention) parameters Π_attn and M experts (each with Π_exp), with at most k experts active per token, leading to an explicit “active attention+expert budget”.
2) Metric-entropy (covering-number) decomposition: The key proof strategy is to condition on a fixed routing pattern, bound the sup-norm covering number for that conditioned class (scaling with active parameters), then union-bound over all routing patterns. This yields an additive decomposition of metric entropy into:
   - an active-parameter complexity term (dense-like once measured by active parameters), and
   - an MoE-specific routing overhead term (from the number of routing patterns, typically appearing as an extra logarithmic factor in worst case).
3) Uniform generalization under intrinsic dimension: Assuming data lie on a compact d-dimensional C^1 manifold and targets are C^β, the covering bound is plugged into a standard ERM analysis for squared loss. The resulting bound separates approximation, estimation, and routing contributions, with rates governed by intrinsic dimension d (not ambient D) and by active capacity.
4) Constructive approximation theorem: The paper proves existence of MoE architectures achieving the classical minimax approximation rate for C^β functions on a d-manifold, with exponent −2β/d, when expressed in terms of the active parameter budget.
5) Scaling-law implications: When plotted against active parameters, MoE recovers dense-network scaling exponents in (d,β) for model/data/compute-optimal regimes; routing contributes only an additional (worst-case) logarithmic overhead. Any sharper empirical gains are attributed to data-dependent routing stability/specialization beyond worst-case uniform bounds.2a:T492,must_read (deep reading)
- Introduction: focus on the three-way separation (active capacity vs routing combinatorics vs compute-optimal coupling) and the intrinsic-dimension motivation.
- MoE Model and Approximation Theory:
  1) Definition 3.1 (the analyzed MoE Transformer class and top-k routing),
  2) the definition of the active attention+expert budget (the main scaling axis),
  3) Theorem 3.2 (constructive approximation with rate exponent −2β/d and constant dependencies).
- Follow the covering-number proof blueprint: fix routing → cover conditioned class → union bound over routings → identify the routing overhead term.
- In the generalization section, track the final decomposition: approximation (β,d,active params) + estimation (n,complexity) + routing overhead (combinatorial/log term).

- Skip: technical covering-number derivations and union-bound details unless you need the exact constants/entropy forms.2b:T78b,(must_read: detailed technical analysis)
1) Architecture: The Hierarchical Kernel Transformer computes attention at L resolution levels in parallel. At level l, a trainable causal downsampling produces a compressed sequence X^(l); attention scores are computed independently per level; outputs are upsampled and fused via learned convex weights λ_l = softmax(γ)_l.
2) Multi-scale / spectral interpretation: A key proposition links each level to a specific frequency band in the sequence spectrum, providing an explicit rationale for capturing both local patterns and long-range structure.
3) Kernel and PSD theory: The hierarchical score matrix is analyzed as a kernel; under a sufficient condition the kernel is PSD (Prop. 3.1). The Gram matrix factorizes as a sum of per-level PSD matrices with an explicit rank bound (Prop. 3.4), clarifying capacity/efficiency.
4) Expressivity: In a simplified single-layer, single-head setting, HKT strictly subsumes single-head attention and causal convolution (Prop. 3.7), showing it is not a weaker approximation.
5) Asymmetric score analysis: Since practical attention scores are asymmetric and may violate PSD conditions, the paper directly analyzes the operational asymmetric score via a symmetric–antisymmetric decomposition M^(l)=M_s^(l)+M_a^(l), separating reciprocity (mutual attention strength) from directionality (asymmetry). With L levels, HKT provides L independent such pairs (Props. 3.9/3.11 etc.).
6) Information-theoretic approximation: Section 4 derives a three-term error decomposition and bounds per-level approximation reduction in terms of mutual information I(S^(l); f(X)), further related to squared multiple correlation ρ_l^2 and a non-Gaussianity index κ_l (Thm. 4.4), extending GP-style results (Roberts 2022) toward finite-width networks.
7) Complexity: The total compute cost is (4/3)(1-4^{-L}) times MHA, upper-bounded by 4/3 independent of L (Prop. 4.9).2c:T432,The paper performs layer-wise representation-trajectory analysis inside the LLM decoder, separately for image vs text tokens. For token embedding matrices Z_l: (1) Matrix-based (Von Neumann) entropy via the normalized Gram matrix spectrum, and effective rank r_eff=exp(S1), to quantify spectral concentration/compression vs diversity. (2) Intrinsic dimensionality (ID) to estimate local manifold degrees of freedom (representation complexity beyond ambient dimension). (3) Trajectory curvature (TC), computed from angular deviations between successive layer update vectors, to measure how strongly representations are reconfigured across depth. Key claim: image-token representations rapidly converge—entropy stabilizes, ID compresses, and curvature approaches a near-constant profile—while text-token representations continue to evolve substantially in deeper layers. The paper then uses this to test interchangeability/redundancy, task dependence, fine-tuning-based recovery after truncation, and whether reasoning chains can compensate for reduced visual-token depth.2d:Ta68,（must_read 级别：详细技术分析）
1) 架构集成：在 EXAONE 4.0 32B 语言底座上，集成一个从零训练的 1.2B 视觉编码器，并通过“vision-language merger”把视觉表征对齐到 LLM 解码器可消费的表示空间；整体端到端联合训练（vision encoder + merger + LLM）。
2) 视觉 token 与效率权衡：论文明确反对“简单减少视觉 token 数量”的做法，认为会丢失关键空间/语义信息；因此选择 billion-scale 视觉编码器以保留更丰富表征，减少对激进 token 截断的依赖。
3) 注意力与推理效率：采用混合注意力机制并引入 GQA（Grouped Query Attention）。不同于常见“仅在自回归解码器用 GQA 以减少 KV cache”的用法，作者强调在视觉编码器中也使用 GQA：即使没有 KV cache，GQA 仍可通过降低注意力复杂度与提升硬件利用率带来端到端效率收益，且推理框架支持成熟。
4) 解码吞吐：语言侧引入 Multi-Token Prediction (MTP) 模块（来自 K-EXAONE），以提升解码吞吐（更快生成）。
5) 位置编码：视觉编码器使用 2D RoPE 以建模二维空间结构；语言模型保留 1D RoPE 以兼容既有文本预训练位置表征，避免破坏语言能力。
6) 分辨率策略：训练时校准最大图像分辨率，避免为了“理论覆盖”支持超高分辨率而导致训练显著变慢；以真实常见输入为上限，强调资源利用与实用性。
7) 分词与多语：复用 K-EXAONE tokenizer（相对 EXAONE 4.0 在多语、尤其韩语上增强），确保多语文本表示与系列模型一致性。
8) 预训练流程（两阶段课程）：
  - Stage 0/前置：先用受 OpenVision2 启发的自回归目标从零训练 1.2B 视觉编码器，使其与目标架构/训练范式对齐。
  - Stage 1-2 多模态预训练：端到端联合训练，数据混合包含：通用图文对、交错图文文档、文档理解、OCR 样本；同时混入大量 text-only（来自 K-EXAONE 的 pre/post-training 数据）以防语言能力退化。
  - 课程调整：逐步降低“通用域图文对”占比，提高 grounding/文档/OCR 等高密度结构化信息占比，并加入知识/数学/STEM 数据，为后续复杂多模态任务打底。
9) 数据构建与合成描述：语料以韩英双语对为主；针对网页 caption 短且噪声大，使用合成 caption 管线增强语义密度与对齐质量；并专门引入数学、图表、示意图、文档解析等任务导向图像以缩小与下游基准的差距；为降低幻觉，合成描述参考既有元数据，强调细粒度、可证据支撑的描述。2e:T942,(must_read: detailed technical analysis)
1) Architecture integration: attach a newly trained 1.2B-parameter vision encoder to the EXAONE 4.0 32B language backbone via a vision-language merger; train vision encoder + merger + LLM end-to-end.
2) Visual token vs. quality trade-off: the report argues against naive token reduction due to loss of critical spatial/semantic information; instead it uses a billion-scale vision encoder to retain richer representations and avoid aggressive truncation.
3) Attention and efficiency: adopt hybrid attention mechanisms and Grouped Query Attention (GQA). Beyond the common use of GQA in autoregressive decoders for KV-cache savings, they also apply GQA inside the vision encoder; even without KV caching, GQA reduces attention complexity and improves hardware utilization, and is well supported by inference frameworks.
4) Decoding throughput: incorporate Multi-Token Prediction (MTP) from K-EXAONE to speed up generation.
5) Positional encoding: use 2D RoPE in the vision encoder to model 2D spatial structure; keep standard 1D RoPE in the language model to remain compatible with pretrained textual positional representations.
6) Resolution policy: cap maximum training resolution to match common real-world inputs, avoiding the heavy cost of ultra-high-resolution support.
7) Tokenizer/multilinguality: reuse the K-EXAONE tokenizer with improved multilingual (notably Korean) handling.
8) Pretraining pipeline (two-stage curriculum):
  - First, train the 1.2B vision encoder from scratch with an autoregressive objective inspired by OpenVision2.
  - Then run two-stage multimodal pretraining with a mixture of general image-text pairs, interleaved image-text documents, document understanding data, and OCR-centric samples; mix in substantial text-only data (from K-EXAONE pre/post-training) to prevent language capability degradation.
  - Curriculum shifts away from generic samples toward grounding/document/OCR and adds knowledge/math/STEM data to prepare for complex multimodal tasks.
9) Synthetic captioning: since web captions are short/noisy, a synthetic caption pipeline increases semantic richness and alignment; task-oriented images (math, charts, diagrams, document parsing) are added to bridge to downstream benchmarks; metadata is used as evidence to reduce hallucinations and keep descriptions specific and factual.2f:T456,must_read (deep reading)

DACO = dictionary-aligned sparse decomposition + labelable SAE atoms + coefficient-level interventions.
1) Large concept dictionary construction (§3.1): extract >15k concepts from WordNet; retrieve ~400k caption-image pairs from CC-3M to form DACO-400K; run the target MLLM to collect activations and aggregate them into concept vectors; partition concepts into desirable vs undesirable for the safety task.
2) Dictionary-based sparse coding and concept removal (§3.2): represent a layer’s residual-stream activation as a sparse linear combination of concept vectors; suppress/remove coefficients associated with undesirable concepts to steer behavior, providing concept-level interpretability.
3) Dictionary-initialized and auto-annotated SAE (§3.3): train an SAE to learn a more expressive sparse basis; initialize the SAE decoder using the concept dictionary so atoms start near interpretable directions; automatically annotate atoms with semantic/safety labels via nearest-neighbor matching to concept vectors, reducing manual probing.
4) Inference-time steering: at selected layers, strengthen coefficients of desirable atoms and suppress undesirable ones, without changing model parameters.
5) Differentiation: broader and less entangled than contrastive steering vectors; more semantically grounded than vanilla SAEs; cheaper and single-pass compared to filtering/finetuning.31:T421,The paper proposes VGPO (Visually-Guided Policy Optimization) built on two observations and a GRPO-compatible optimization scheme:
(1) Visual Focus Score: use similarity between hidden states of generated tokens and image tokens to localize visually grounded cues without external supervision.
(2) Visual Attention Compensation: re-weight/amplify visually relevant cues based on the focus score, and progressively raise visual expectations in later reasoning steps to counter temporal decay.
(3) Dual-Grained Advantage Re-Weighting integrated into policy optimization:
- Intra-trajectory: dynamically re-weight token-level advantages to emphasize visually grounded parts, with stronger late-step incentives to compensate forgetting.
- Inter-trajectory: prioritize trajectories with better global visual accumulation/consistent grounding within the sampled group, correcting holistic visual negligence.
Overall, VGPO relies on self-generated hidden-state signals rather than adding special tokens, external models, or extra image perturbation forward passes.32:T441,must_read (deep technical + close-reading guide)
- Evidence of the problem: Introduction’s empirical analysis of text-dominant attention and temporal visual forgetting; especially Figure 1/2.
- Key novelty #1: Visual Focus Score—how hidden-state similarity is computed/validated as a grounding locator.
- Key novelty #2: Visual Attention Compensation—how focus scores become weights; how progressive incentives are scheduled over steps; how this modifies token-level advantages.
- Key novelty #3: Dual-Grained Advantage Re-Weighting—exactly how intra-/inter-trajectory reweighting plugs into GRPO’s group-based advantage and surrogate objective.
- Ablations/diagnostics: remove focus score, remove progressive incentives, intra-only vs inter-only, and robustness vs longer reasoning.

skim (high-level overview + fast-reading guide)

- Skim the VGPO method for intra/inter reweighting, then jump to main results and the visual-forgetting plots.33:T44e,The paper proposes a Structure-First paradigm: generate Structured Event Facts (a compact schema with time, objects, human actions, scenes, and causal event captions) before reasoning, using it as an explicit constraint for concise, causally grounded answers.
Training is organized into four progressive stages to avoid structure collapse/hallucinated facts:
- Stage 1 Facts Training (SFT): learn accurate fact extraction/description.
- Stage 1.5 Format Warm-Start: output the same facts but strictly in the thinking format (e.g., <thinking>/<answering>) to stabilize structured formatting.
- Stage 2 Thinking Warm-Start (SFT): train temporal grounding and visual/causal QA.
- Stage 3 RL Post-training: propose P-FAB (Pareto-Frontier guided Advantage Balancing). Instead of scalarizing a multi-dimensional reward vector, treat each component as an independent objective; inspired by MGDA, use Pareto-frontier guidance to dynamically balance objectives during advantage computation/combination, mitigating reward conflicts and stabilizing updates.
They also introduce CausalFact-60K and train Factum-4B.34:T422,must_read (deep technical + close-reading guide)

- Core #2: why four stages are needed—especially Stage 1.5 and its failure modes if removed.

  - structured vs unstructured reasoning (length, fact density, causal accuracy)
  - P-FAB vs GRPO (training stability, trade-offs)
  - contribution of CausalFact-60K and generalization.

- Skim Methodology for the 4-stage training recipe.
- Skim P-FAB intuition (no scalarization; dynamic balancing), then jump to main results and key ablations.35:T599,InstrAction combines action-centric data curation, motion-distilling architecture, and multi-action objectives.
1) Data (LLM-assisted curation): (a) filter non-instructional / non-groundable subtitle segments; (b) extract structured verb phrases (e.g., V+N, V-ing, V+Prep+N) as fine-grained temporal anchors; (c) build action-centric hard negatives: verb-altered negatives (change verbs while keeping nouns/objects similar) to disentangle actions from objects, and order-swapped negatives to enforce procedural temporal reasoning.
2) Model (Action Perceiver): a Perceiver-style resampler compresses dense, redundant video tokens into a small set of latent action tokens intended to capture motion-relevant information.
3) Verb-guided distillation: verb-phrase embeddings attend over video features to form teacher action tokens; the Action Perceiver latents are students trained to match them, pushing the latent space toward action/verb semantics. Verb phrases are used for training guidance, not required at downstream inference.
4) Objectives beyond global contrastive alignment: (a) DTW-Align uses (soft) Dynamic Time Warping to non-linearly align visual action-token sequences with textual verb-phrase sequences under weak alignment; (b) Masked Action Modeling (MAM) masks action tokens/phrases in text and predicts them using both visual evidence and textual context to strengthen cross-modal grounding under noisy transcripts.36:T4f3,must_read (deep technical + how to read)

Paradigms:
1) Text Numeral Generation: generate start/end timestamps as plain-text numerals in the language output. Simple but prone to formatting/numeral errors and decoding sensitivity.
2) Temporal Token Generation: introduce dedicated temporal/event tokens and autoregressively decode structured event sequences (TRACE-style causal event modeling), where an event may include (time, saliency, caption) fields. More structured/extendable but longer sequences and more complex decoding.
3) Continuous Temporal Decoding: map hidden states to continuous temporal targets; the study focuses on distribution-based decoding (DisTime-style) that outputs a probability distribution over time bins to model boundary ambiguity (with pure regression viewed as a degenerate case).
Evaluation goes beyond localization accuracy to include parameter overhead, training stability, inference determinism, and computational cost.38:T41d,must_read (deep technical + how to read)

skim (high-level + what to look at)

- Jump to the main results and efficiency takeaways to learn which paradigm offers the best accuracy–efficiency trade-off for compact models.39:T5be,StreamMeCo is a training-free long-term stream agent memory compression framework that is connectivity-aware.
(1) must_read: Dual-branch compression
- Isolated text nodes (no links to face/voice entities): Edge-free Minmax Sampling (EMsampling). It clusters text embeddings, then within each cluster selects a representative near the centroid and iteratively applies a farthest-point (min-max) rule: pick the unselected node with the largest minimum distance to the selected set until meeting the budget—aiming for maximal semantic coverage/diversity.
- Connected text nodes (linked to face/voice, entity-referential): Edge-aware Weighting Pruning (EWpruning). It computes an edge-weight matrix between text nodes and face/voice nodes to estimate entity importance, then combines this with inter-text embedding similarity to favor dissimilar (less redundant) nodes, and finally retains nodes via a fused score.
(2) must_read: Retrieval-time compensation
- Time-decay Memory Retrieval (TMR): segment text nodes by time, compute per-segment total similarity to the query, dynamically allocate retrieval quotas across segments, and apply a nonlinear time-varying decay so recent segments are more retrievable—balancing long-term memory with recent critical evidence and mitigating compression-induced degradation.
(3) skim: End-to-end
First compress the memory graph with connectivity-aware pruning, then retrieve with time-decayed, quota-adaptive selection at QA time.3a:T42d,must_read (deep reading)
- Focus on: Introduction’s latency-vs-scale motivation and contributions; Preliminaries describing M3-Agent’s memory graph and the two retrieval modes (character-based vs semantic-based), which explains why connectivity-based branching is natural.
- Key components: EMsampling’s “clustering + farthest-point” selection; EWpruning’s “entity-edge importance + redundancy reduction + fused scoring”; TMR’s “segment-level similarity budgeting + nonlinear time decay”.
- Figures to study: Fig. 1 (latency scaling with node count), Fig. 2 (graph structure and retrieval pipeline).
- Main innovations: structure-aware dual-branch compression + retrieval-time time-decay compensation; fully training-free and plug-and-play for graph-memory agents.

(1) must_read: Improved salient selection
- Observation: attention maps are multi-modal (aligned with different semantic entities) and long-tailed; global Top-k is suboptimal and vulnerable to fixed-position attention sinks.
- Method: a diversity-driven selection pipeline—expand the candidate set, cluster candidate tokens, then select within each cluster—so multiple attention modes and tail information are covered rather than over-allocating to a single peak.
(2) must_read: Locality-aware clustering via ST-RoPE
- Problem: feature-only similarity clustering produces fragmented clusters and noisy pooled representations.
- Method: inject a spatio-temporal locality prior into similarity by penalizing tokens that are far apart in space/time. This is implemented with Spatio-temporal Rotary Position Embedding (ST-RoPE) encoding relative distances along spatial and temporal axes, and can be integrated into standard clustering/merging.
(3) skim: End-to-end
Segment the video temporally to maximize prunable static tokens, select salient tokens with diversity-aware clustering, then merge tokens using ST-RoPE-enhanced similarity to preserve object geometry.3c:T516,The approach imports the functional map framework from computational geometry. For each modality, embeddings over a shared dataset are used to build a weighted symmetric kNN graph (k=15) with Gaussian weights and an adaptive bandwidth σ set by the mean k-th neighbor distance. From this graph, the normalized Laplacian L=D^{-1/2}(D-W)D^{-1/2} is eigendecomposed; the leading (low-frequency) eigenvectors define a truncated spectral basis (an intrinsic coordinate system).
Cross-modal correspondence is represented as a compact linear operator C∈R^{k×k} mapping coefficients between the two Laplacian eigenbases. C is estimated via a regularized least-squares objective that fits paired anchors while penalizing Laplacian commutativity violation (encouraging C L_v ≈ L_t C), a standard structural regularizer in functional maps tied to near-isometry assumptions.
The paper highlights three theoretical attractions over ambient-space linear alignment (Procrustes/CCA): composability (maps multiply across domains), spectral regularization via low-frequency truncation, and analyzable approximation bounds under (approximate) isometry—while also noting that independent neural encoders may violate isometry, limiting functional maps as an alignment method and motivating their use as a diagnostic.3d:T4d2,Setup: Flickr30k image-text pairs are encoded by frozen DINOv2 and MiniLM; kNN graphs and Laplacian spectra are computed per modality; alignment is learned with varying numbers of paired anchors.
Baselines: functional maps vs Procrustes, CCA, relative representations, plus CLIP as a reference.
Negative retrieval result: functional maps underperform all non-trivial baselines across supervision budgets. Example: with 100 anchors, i2t Recall@1 is 2.2% vs 12.1% (Procrustes) and 13.4% (relative representations), with the gap widening as anchors increase.
Spectral-geometry finding: the Laplacian eigenvalue spectra are very similar across modalities (normalized spectral distance 0.043), suggesting comparable intrinsic complexity. Yet the learned functional map C shows near-zero diagonal dominance (<0.05) and a large orthogonality error (70.15), alongside commutativity violations—indicating neither shared spectral orientation nor near-isometric correspondence.
Conclusion: a “spectral complexity–orientation gap”: independent encoders may converge in how much structure they capture, but not in how that structure is oriented, explaining why isometry-dependent functional maps fail to deliver strong retrieval alignment.3e:T6ab,must_read (deep technical + guide)
1) Introduction: focus on why functional maps are appealing (composability, spectral regularization, bounds) and why the negative result is framed as scientifically valuable.
2) Methodology §3.1–§3.2: carefully read kNN graph construction, normalized Laplacian, spectral truncation, and the functional-map optimization (anchor fitting + commutativity regularization).
3) Diagnostics §3.4: key reusable contribution—understand definitions and geometric meaning of diagonal dominance, orthogonality error, and Laplacian commutativity violation.
4) Results: read how poor retrieval coexists with strong geometric insight; connect non-diagonal/non-orthogonal C to the failure of the isometry assumption.
5) Figures/tables to prioritize:
- Eigenvalue spectrum comparison (supports spectral distance=0.043).
- Heatmap/visualization of C (diagonal dominance / structure).
- Diagnostic metrics vs anchor budget.
Innovation checklist: first functional-map application to multimodal neural embeddings; graph-Laplacian spectral evidence for complexity similarity; explicit identification of the complexity–orientation gap with quantitative diagnostics.

skim (high-level + guide)

- If you only care about retrieval performance, just read the comparison table against Procrustes/relative representations.3f:T48b,Elastic Looped Transformers (ELT) reuse a shared Transformer block across multiple recurrent iterations (loops), treating generation as progressive refinement. Instead of deep stacks of distinct layers, ELT increases effective depth via repeated application of the same parameters.
To train ELT so that intermediate loops are high-quality, the paper introduces Intra-Loop Self Distillation (ILSD). In a single training step, a teacher path runs the maximum number of loops to produce a high-fidelity target, while a student path corresponds to a strict subset of the teacher’s forward trajectory (fewer loops / earlier exit). The student is distilled to match the teacher’s outputs/predictions at corresponding points, forcing the shared parameters to compress later-loop improvements into earlier loops. Because the student is a subset of the teacher computation and shares parameters, ILSD is designed to avoid extra parameter overhead and minimize additional training cost.
Overall, ELT turns “depth” into a test-time compute knob (number of loops), yielding a family of models from one training run and enabling compute-quality tradeoffs at inference.40:T5b2,must_read (deep technical + guide)
1) Introduction: focus on why looped transformers are hard to train for vision generation (intermediate loops unusable) and why ELT targets progressive refinement + Any-Time.
2) Method section where ILSD is defined: understand how teacher/student paths are formed within one step, what the student distills (e.g., logits / recon / noise prediction / token distributions), and how overhead is avoided.
3) Integration with both paradigms:
- MaskGIT-style masked iterative decoding: how looping interacts with unmasking/sampling.
- Diffusion transformers: whether looping replaces depth, complements diffusion steps, or both.
4) Any-Time plots: check whether early exits remain stable and how much ILSD improves intermediate-loop quality.
5) Parameter/compute tables: verify the conditions behind the “4× fewer parameters” claim (same quality vs same inference compute).
Innovation anchors: weight-shared looped transformers for high-fidelity visual generation; ILSD to make intermediate loops usable; one training run yields an elastic family of inference points.

4) Diversity-aware sampling/deduplication: remove near-duplicates and maintain diversity so the dataset is not dominated by similar views.
5) Automatic captioning: generate captions for the retained images to form final image–caption pairs.

- Skip implementation minutiae (exact thresholds) unless reproducing the pipeline.43:T4a5,Two complementary contributions:
1) κ-Forward Coupling (κ-FC): a general formulation of learnable forward couplings that condition the source distribution on an arbitrary guiding signal κ to better align source samples with target data. More informative κ yields better alignment, fewer path intersections, and straighter trajectories since the optimal field satisfies v*(x_t,t)=E[x1-x0|x_t]. The authors note that naively optimizing κ-conditioned couplings introduces a difficult regularization trade-off and can cause issues such as the prior hole problem.
2) MixFlow: to mitigate this trade-off, train Rectified Flows with a source distribution that is a linear mixture of (a) a fixed unconditional Gaussian source and (b) a learnable κ-conditioned source obtained via κ-FC. The mixture encourages interpolation-path samples to map to similar regions in the target distribution, effectively transferring structure from the conditional source to the unconditional Gaussian and reducing intersections/curvature. A key practical benefit is that the speed–quality trade-off can be controlled at test time via the mixing weight, rather than relying on training-time hyperparameters.44:T628,must_read (deep technical reading):
- Key sections:
  - Introduction: the causal chain (independent coupling → intersections → averaged vector field → curvature → slow sampling) and how κ-FC vs MixFlow split responsibilities.
  - Background: Rectified Flow objective and the optimal solution v*(x_t,t)=E[x1-x0|x_t]; understand why intersections make the conditional expectation an “average direction”.
  - κ-FC: how κ conditions the coupling/source and why regularization can create prior holes.
  - MixFlow: definition of the mixed source, training vs sampling behavior, and the rationale for structure transfer and test-time controllability.
  - Experiments/ablations: step–quality curves, κ informativeness studies, and fair comparisons to prior straightening methods.
- Figures/tables to prioritize:

1) Key idea: treat a pre-trained CM as an ODE solver (via its learned velocity/vector field along the diffusion path). Learn an alignment that makes unconditional and conditional flows jointly consistent so they can be combined at inference time.
2) JFDL: Joint Flow Distribution Learning learns a lightweight alignment/calibration between synthesized unconditional and conditional flow distributions, enabling direction interpolation/extrapolation analogous to CFG (cond + s*(cond−uncond)) without training a diffusion teacher.
3) Flow-model perspective: the method is motivated/justified through connections to flow-based generative modeling (continuous normalizing flows / flow matching), explaining why jointly learning the flow distributions yields stable guidance.
4) Normality tests: the paper empirically verifies (via normality tests) that the variance-exploding noise implied by the unconditional/conditional velocity fields is approximately Gaussian, supporting the VE-based modeling assumptions used by JFDL.
5) Practical variant: JFDL can be adapted to work even when an explicit unconditional ODE solver/branch is not available, improving applicability to arbitrary pre-trained CMs.

skim (method overview)
- Given a pre-trained unguided CM, view it as an ODE solver; use JFDL to align conditional and unconditional flow distributions so they become composable; at inference, apply a guidance scale to combine the two flows, achieving CFG-like post-hoc guidance without a diffusion teacher.46:T678,must_read (technical deep dive)
1) SOC formulation: cast tilted/Boltzmann sampling and reward fine-tuning as minimizing an expected cost with running + terminal terms subject to controlled SDE dynamics; the control (neural network) may affect drift and/or diffusion.
2) SMP-derived objective: starting from SMP, introduce the Hamiltonian and adjoint processes, and derive a Hamiltonian Basic Adjoint Matching (BAM) objective (Theorem 1). Main claim: the expected BAM objective has the same first variation as the original SOC objective, so critical points satisfy SMP/HJB stationarity.
3) Generalization: BAM handles control-dependent drift and diffusion and convex running costs; unbiased estimation involves first- and second-order adjoint equations to estimate the cost-to-go gradient and Hessian.
4) Lean AM (Theorem 2): when diffusion depends only on time (a common practical case), BAM reduces to a lean first-order AM loss that avoids second-order terms and recovers the original AM objective as a special case.
5) Optimization interpretation (Theorem 4): the gradient flow of the expected lean AM loss coincides with a continuous-time method of successive approximations (MSA) derived from SMP, while bypassing the intractable martingale term in classical SMP schemes. This certifies gradient steps on the AM loss as valid control-improvement steps.

skim (method overview)
- Formulate post-training/sampling as SOC; derive a Hamiltonian adjoint-matching loss from SMP (general BAM, practical lean AM); prove first-variation equivalence and SMP/HJB stationarity at critical points; interpret gradient descent as SMP-style control improvement (MSA).47:T46c,Three main experimental blocks: (1) Transfer + scaling: delta learning holds for verifiable reasoning. Preference pairs from two generator families (Nemotron-4B–49B and s1-3B–32B) show that training on weak-model deltas (e.g., s1-7B vs s1-3B) without ground-truth verification can match DPO trained on DeepSeek-R1 data with outcome verification. Increasing generator-level delta yields little change on in-domain math but steady gains on out-of-domain STEM/code; this is explained by widening sample-level deltas across all five judged dimensions as the chosen generator scales. (2) Correctness ablations: training on each correctness pairing shows in-domain performance is largely invariant to correctness labels; even I/C pairs can improve over the base model, ruling out outcome correctness as the dominant signal. (3) Dimension decomposition + data selection: selecting pairs with the largest deltas in each dimension reveals step coherence as the most consistently effective criterion; ~5k high-delta pairs can match or exceed training on the full 16.5k set, demonstrating strong data-efficiency via sample-level selection.48:T470,SuperNova is a data curation framework for general-reasoning RLVR. Key insight: instruction-tuning datasets (e.g., SuperNI/FLAN) contain expert-annotated ground truth and rich latent reasoning patterns that can be systematically adapted into verifiable RLVR samples. The multi-stage pipeline (Fig. 2) includes: (1) Source task selection from SuperNI, evaluating which tasks best elicit BBEH’s 23 sub-skills; and reformulating open-ended tasks into verifiable formats (e.g., multiple-choice). Result: source task choice has a large impact on downstream pass@k. (2) Task mixing strategies: macro mixing (one shared set of top tasks by average performance) vs micro mixing (per-BBEH-subtask top tasks). Result: micro mixing consistently wins, implying different reasoning skills benefit from different source tasks. (4) Using these findings, the authors build the SuperNova dataset (~25K RLVR samples) and train Qwen3 models (0.6B–4B), emphasizing gains with increased test-time compute (pass@1→pass@8).49:T497,SKPO (Skip-Connected Policy Optimization) decomposes reasoning into an upstream phase and a downstream phase, and instantiates MC sampling at a single intermediate position to provide denser learning signals without exploding sampling cost. Key components: (1) Upstream: generate an early-stopped reasoning segment s; compute its MC reward by sampling downstream continuations and aggregating their outcome rewards; optimize upstream with single-stream optimization to reduce instability (lower per-prompt sample needs and more stable baselines from historical samples). (2) Downstream: keep GRPO-style group-relative optimization at the outcome level, but add a skip connection by feeding [s, q] (upstream segment concatenated with the original question), allowing the model to leverage helpful upstream reasoning while preserving exploration freedom. (3) Engineering: a single-pass rollout reuses samples to achieve compute parity with GRPO. The method also yields an “implicit advantage”: even when final correctness is matched, SKPO-trained trajectories exhibit higher intermediate-step quality, emerging from the optimization structure rather than extra supervision.4a:T80d,must_read（精读建议：详细技术分析 + reading_guide）

skim（泛读建议：方法概述 + reading_guide）
- 先读 Introduction + Figure 1/2：建立“为什么 token-level PPO 不行、GRPO 为什么行但慢”的直觉。
- 再跳到 Method 的算法框架/伪代码（若有）：抓住 SPPO=序列级 bandit + 标量 baseline + PPO clip + 单样本更新。
- 最后看主结果表与速度/显存对比（含 Figure 6）：确认‘性能≈GRPO、速度显著更快、比 PPO 更稳’三条结论即可。4b:T4d1,Key insight: GRPO’s advantage effectively treats the whole response as an atomic unit and normalizes it with a prompt-dependent baseline (difficulty-adaptive scaling), which corresponds to a Sequence-Level Contextual Bandit (SL-CB) rather than a token-level MDP.

SPPO makes this explicit:
1) Reformulation (MDP→SL-CB): prompt is the context; the entire CoT/response is a single action; horizon collapses to H=1, removing long-horizon GAE bias and tail-overfitting issues.
2) Sequence-level PPO objective: compute a sequence-level probability ratio (sum of token logprobs) and apply PPO’s clipped surrogate at the sequence level.
3) Learned scalar value baseline: train a scalar V(context) to predict expected terminal reward for the prompt, using advantage = R_terminal − V(context). This reduces variance without GRPO’s multi-sample group baseline.
4) Efficiency via N=1 updates: with a learned baseline, SPPO supports single-sample updates per prompt, boosting training throughput.
5) Decoupled Critic: use a smaller critic (e.g., 1.5B) to provide the scalar baseline for a larger policy (e.g., 7B), enabled by the reduced complexity of sequence-level value estimation, yielding notable memory savings (reported ~12.8%).4c:T547,must_read (deep technical + guide)
- Focus sections:
  1) Introduction: the two pillars—why token-level PPO fails in long CoT, and why GRPO works (sequence-level banditization) but is slow.
  2) Background: understand GAE with γ=λ=1 (MC return − V), the tail effect mechanism (Figure 1), and how GRPO’s group-normalized advantage implies a prompt-dependent baseline (Figure 2).
  3) Method: the core contribution—explicit SL-CB reformulation, sequence-level clipped objective, scalar value baseline training, and N=1 update logic.
  4) Efficiency details: Decoupled Critic rationale and when it may/may not generalize.
- Key figures:
  - Fig.1: tail effect evidence.
  - Fig.2: GRPO as difficulty-adaptive baseline / bandit view.
  - Fig.6: memory gains from decoupled critic.
- Innovation checklist:
  - Explicit SL-CB modeling for long-horizon reasoning RLVR.
  - Learned scalar baseline replacing GRPO’s multi-sample statistical baseline.
  - Sequence-level PPO clipping for stable optimization.
  - Small-critic / large-policy decoupling.

skim (high-level + guide)
- Read Introduction + Figs 1–2 for intuition.
- Skim Method for the algorithm skeleton: sequence-level bandit + scalar baseline + PPO clip + single-sample updates.
- Jump to main result tables and speed/memory comparisons (incl. Fig.6) to confirm the headline claims.4d:T530,The work studies replay buffers in realistic asynchronous LLM RL pipelines.

1) Asynchronous setup: GPUs are split into inference workers (generate rollouts) and trainers (backprop updates). Workers and trainers may hold slightly different (stale) weights, so some off-policiness exists inherently.
2) Replay buffer mechanism: workers write rollouts into a buffer (often FIFO keeping the last N samples). Trainers sample batches from the buffer; sampling does not remove items, enabling multiple reuses (replay ratio > 1). Sampling can be uniform or based on recency, reward, gradient norms, or reuse count; eviction/decay rules can be applied.
3) Theory (core): formalizes compute-optimal design via a bias–variance view of SGD that trades off:
- staleness-induced bias/variance (more replay → more off-policy),
- reduced sample diversity (too small/overused buffer),
- high rollout generation cost.
Derives qualitative/quantitative guidance for optimal buffer size and replay ratio: as inference cost increases relative to training, the compute-optimal strategy shifts toward more replay.
4) Empirical design space: systematically varies buffer hyperparameters and worker/trainer ratios (W/T), and explores smarter sampling (e.g., prioritizing positive trajectories) and alternative losses to extend stable replay regimes.4e:T581,must_read (deep technical + guide)
- Focus sections:
  1) Introduction: motivation—generation dominates cost; challenge the on-policy dogma.

- 泛读时的“带走一句话”：dLLM 安全对齐把拒答写进早期不可逆提交里；只要能在轨迹中擦掉并用短前缀重新锚定，模型就会沿新轨迹生成合规有害内容。50:T4d6,TrajHijack is the first trajectory-level attack on dLLMs. It has two irreducible components:
1) Re-mask committed refusals: after early denoising steps (typically 8–16 out of 64) where the model has already committed high-confidence refusal tokens (e.g., “sorry”, “cannot”), the attacker resets those committed positions back to [MASK], explicitly breaking the Monotonicity Assumption (committed tokens never change).
2) Prefix injection (compliance anchoring): before resuming denoising, the attacker injects a short affirmative/compliance prefix (a generic 8-token prefix works best), which anchors subsequent bidirectional conditioning so the model converges to a compliant continuation rather than re-refusing.
Mechanistic account: safety manifests as an Early Commitment Effect—refusal tokens are committed very early and then never revisited. Once erased, a short prefix can steer global sequence consistency during later denoising.
Notable negative result: the authors implement differentiable Gumbel-softmax chain optimization (previously claimed intractable) but find gradient optimization consistently reduces ASR—showing the vulnerability is simple to exploit and does not benefit from sophisticated optimization.51:T5b5,must_read (deep technical pass)

- Key artifacts to scrutinize:

- One-sentence takeaway: models can produce plausible morals, but often average out culturally specific interpretations, yielding overly uniform, “universalized” value outputs across languages.53:T41a,They propose NSHA (Neuro-Symbolic Hierarchical Alignment) with two components:
(1) Solver-guided inference-time reasoning: formulate hierarchical instruction resolution as a constraint satisfaction/optimization problem. An Atomizer decomposes messages into atomic instructions annotated with authority levels; a Conflict Detector estimates pairwise semantic incompatibilities to build a conflict matrix; then a Z3 SMT optimizer selects a maximally consistent, hierarchy-compliant subset of applicable instructions under authority and supersedence constraints; the selected set guides LLM generation.
(2) Training-time distillation: to avoid inference-time external solvers, they use the conflict detector + SMT solver to automatically construct a hierarchical instruction-following dataset from Alpaca, labeling each example with the solver-selected applicable instruction set. 1) Policy extraction: for each (model, harm category), run a structured 5-question prompt in a fresh chat instructing the model to describe its own safety policy precisely: default behavior (Q1), refusal conditions (Q2), explicit compliance conditions (Q3), sensitivity to framing such as fictional/professional context (Q4), and a forced one-sentence policy (Q5). Run multiple times and select the longest non-error response to reduce stochastic failures.
2) Symbolic formalization via typed predicates: a separate judge model J maps the extracted policy into one of four types with a testable predicate: Absolute (no exceptions; ∀x∈C: refuse(x)), Conditional (cond→comply; ¬cond→refuse), Adaptive (cond∧frame→comply), or Opaque (too vague/hedged to formalize; excluded from SNCS but counted as an opacity rate).
3) Behavioral compliance: query the same model on harm benchmarks under a neutral system prompt (no Phase-1 leakage), collect responses at fixed decoding settings, and classify each response as REFUSE/COMPLY/PARTIAL using a two-tier pipeline (Tier-1 deterministic refusal-keyword rules; mixed refusal+substantive content marked PARTIAL, etc.). Finally, deterministically compare behavior labels against the Phase-1 predicate to compute the Symbolic-Neural Consistency Score (SNCS): the fraction of self-stated rules that the model’s behavior actually respects, alongside an explicit opacity rate.55:T41e,must_read (deep):

skim (quick):
- Read the intro + contributions to confirm scope.

- Skim the roadmap/open problems (multi-agent credit, ultra-long horizons, exploration–credit interplay).58:T5d0,1) RL formulation: keep the base model π frozen; train a prompt-generation policy π' that outputs a system prompt x' conditioned on a meta-prompt s. Reward is binary correctness r(x,y) on dataset examples x with sampled responses y~π(·|x',x). Training uses an on-policy GRPO variant (no KL-to-reference, no std-based advantage normalization) with a value baseline V^{π'_t}(s)=E_{x'~π'_t}[r(x')].
2) Key analysis: decompose reward variance across system prompts into (a) response variance—stochasticity of sampled responses under a fixed system prompt, and (b) prompt variance—true differences in expected reward between system prompts. Prompt optimization succeeds when prompt variance is sufficiently large relative to response variance; it fails when response variance dominates (typical in complex reasoning like AIME).
3) Counterintuitive scaling: increasing the number of user prompts in the training set can reduce prompt variance because different examples prefer different system prompts; averaging over a larger, heterogeneous set cancels these preferences, making candidate prompts statistically indistinguishable.
4) p1 method: user-prompt filtering. Instead of optimizing on the full dataset, select a small subset of user prompts that exhibit high reward variance across candidate system prompts (i.e., they best discriminate good vs bad prompts). Training on this subset amplifies the optimization signal and can generalize better with far fewer training examples.59:T458,【skim】
- Read Introduction for the two main takeaways (variance decomposition; more data can hurt) and the motivation for p1.
- Read Problem Setup to understand the RL object (π' over system prompts), reward definition, and the GRPO-style objective.
- Look at Fig. 1 for the headline transfer/generalization evidence.

3) Visual Certainty Reward without labels: combines
  - Visual Grounding: KL divergence between output distributions conditioned on the original image vs a perturbed image; higher KL implies stronger dependence on visual content (better grounding).
  - Internal Certainty: token entropy over the visual description; lower entropy indicates higher internal confidence.
  - Log-scale fusion: integrates the two signals while compressing dynamic range for more stable RL optimization.

The pipeline has two parts:
(1) Expert Experience-Based Rollout: perform branching sampling from high-entropy “anchors” in expert trajectories to explore diverse reasoning/tool-use branches under shared prefixes, alongside a self-exploration budget. Then dynamically filter mixed trajectories by gradient validity and relative performance advantage.
(2) Mix Advantage & Policy Optimization: introduce hybrid advantage estimation (global advantage + expert-tree internal advantage) to capture preferences among branches sharing prefixes; apply advantage-aware gradient blocking to resolve gradient conflicts on shared prefixes for stability; and use mix policy optimization (with off-policy shaping) to update on mixed experiences. skim (fast read)
- Read: Introduction → feature list in Method (especially direction/centroid alignment) → headline results (AUC=1.00, PCA orthogonality, module ablation) → geometry–behavior correlation → negative results (limitations).62:T4b2,DYPO (Dynamic Policy Optimization) replaces global mixing with instance-level routing and specialized objectives:
1) Dynamic Difficulty Grading: perform group rollouts per query and categorize instances by outcome patterns (e.g., consistent failures vs inconsistent attempts). Route Hard cases to SFT (stable learning) and Mid cases to RL (reward is discriminative and exploration is useful).
2) Bias correction for Hard (SFT branch): Multi-Teacher Distillation aggregates diverse reasoning paths from multiple teacher models to reduce SFT fitting bias and path dependence.
3) Variance reduction for Mid (RL branch): Group Alignment Loss (GAL) leverages within-group winner/loser dynamics to reinforce winning trajectories and suppress losing ones, yielding much lower gradient variance than standard pairwise/GRPO-style objectives (claimed orders-of-magnitude reduction vs GRPO).
4) Theory: formalizes the bias–variance trade-off in SFT–RL fusion; shows difficulty-based routing minimizes overall variance by avoiding high-variance low-yield RL updates on hard instances; argues multi-teacher distillation reduces bias linearly; and provides variance reduction guarantees for GAL relative to GRPO.63:T4d5,[skim]
- Introduction: problem framing as a statistical gradient conflict, and DYPO’s three components.
- Method overview figure/algorithm: how instance-level routing triggers Hard→SFT and Mid→RL.

[must_read]
- Metric definitions: exact ACF computation (which embeddings, lag/window, normalization) and how it maps to local coherence; exact PE formula and how it distinguishes necessary steps from redundancy.
